38:1680:82495:12:12:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:The quantitative model has 11 one-digit occupation groups.  For graphical representation only, we re-group
38:1681:82601:12:12:72:540:518:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:the 10 worker occupations into the 3 broader categories of manual, routine, and abstract as in Table 3.  The
38:1682:82709:12:12:72:540:506:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:vertical axes are the ratios between the average wages of occupation groups, manual-to-routine on the left
38:1683:82815:12:34:72:207:494:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:and all the others on the right.
38:1684:82847:34:18:101:510:460:12:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f5:Relative wagesAlthough we target the 1980 average wages of the 11 occupation
38:1685:82923:18:18:101:510:442:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:groups in our calibration, we do not exploit any other wage-related moments.  Before
38:1686:83007:18:18:101:510:424:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:we further discuss the model’s implications for wage moments,  two clarifications are
38:1687:83092:18:17:101:510:406:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:in order.  First,  in our model,  individuals’ earnings depends only on their skills and
38:1688:83180:17:18:101:510:389:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:occupation, not on sectors.  In the data, the average wage even for a narrowly-defined
38:1689:83266:18:18:101:510:371:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:occupation  is  somewhat  higher  in  manufacturing  than  in  services—for  example,  see
38:1690:83356:18:18:101:510:353:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:Appendix Figure 23(b).34We do not directly address this fact, and the average wages
38:1691:83439:18:18:101:510:335:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:of broad occupation groups (e.g., workers as a whole or the manual, routine, abstract
38:1692:83524:18:18:101:510:317:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:categories) are different between the two sectors only to the extent that they differ in
38:1693:83612:18:18:101:454:299:11:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:how intensely they use the 10 underlying worker occupations in Table 3.35
38:1694:83685:18:18:117:511:281:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:Second, Proposition 2 leads to polarization cast in terms of wage per skill (wzand
38:1695:83767:18:18:101:510:263:11:g_d0_f14:11:g_d0_f5:wh).  Our unit of observation is now wages, which equal (wage per skill)×(amount of
38:1696:83850:18:18:101:510:245:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:skill).   Accordingly,  the  average  wage  of  an  occupation  changes  not  only  because  of
38:1697:83945:18:18:101:488:227:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:changes in its per-skill wage, but also because of selection on skill by occupation.
38:1698:84029:18:18:117:510:209:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:Figure  12  plots  the  relative  mean  wages  of  occupations  (in  aggregate).   Manual
38:1699:84118:18:18:101:510:191:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:and  abstract  wages  are  relative  to  routine  jobs,  and  manager  wages  are  relative  to
38:1700:84213:18:18:101:510:173:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:all  workers.   While  the  model  trends  are  qualitatively  consistent  with  the  data,  i.e.,
38:1701:84311:18:18:101:510:155:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:horizontal and vertical wage polarization, the quantitative fit is not tight. In particular,
38:1702:84403:18:26:101:510:137:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:the rise in the relative wage of the manager and abstract occupations is more muted
38:1703:84486:26:16:84:540:111:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f5:34However, the sectoral difference in the average wage of an occupation is stable throughout the observa-
38:1704:84591:16:8:72:439:95:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:tion period, so we consider the indifference assumption to be valid up to a constant.
38:1705:84676:8:15:84:540:87:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f5:35In this version of the model with discretized worker occupations, we only consider those equilibria in
38:1706:84780:15:0:72:365:72:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:which mean skill levels within occupations are equal across sectors.

39:1716:85255:34:18:101:510:463:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:than in the data, which is explained bynegativeselection.  As shown by Proposition
39:1717:85337:18:18:101:510:445:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:2  and  Figure  8,  barring  a  change  in  the  underlying  distributions  of  skills,  vertical
39:1718:85434:18:18:101:510:427:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:polarization dictates that new managers have less managerial skillzthan existing ones.
39:1719:85520:18:18:101:510:409:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:This brings down the mean skill level of managers, countering the positive impact on
39:1720:85604:18:18:101:510:391:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:the average wage of managers coming from the rise in wage per managerial skillwz.
39:1721:85685:18:18:101:510:373:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:Likewise,  horizontal  polarization  necessarily  implies  lower-hworkers  in  the  highest-
39:1722:85777:18:18:101:510:355:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:paying abstract jobs, attenuating the positive impact on their average wage from the
39:1723:85861:18:33:101:290:337:11:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:higher wage per skill (wjforj= 8,9).36
39:1724:85899:33:18:101:510:304:12:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f5:Average size of establishmentsIn our model, the production technology has
39:1725:85972:18:18:101:510:286:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:constant returns to scale, and the size distribution of establishments is not pinned down.
39:1726:86062:18:18:101:510:268:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:However,  because we model managers as a special occupation qualitatively separate
39:1727:86144:18:18:101:510:250:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:from workers, the model does have implications on the average size of establishments
39:1728:86228:18:18:101:510:232:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:as long as we assume a stable relationship between managers and establishments.  With
39:1729:86313:18:18:101:510:214:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:such an assumption, e.g., a constant number of managers per establishment over time,
39:1730:86397:18:18:101:510:196:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:the faster vertical polarization in manufacturing, Figure 5(b), implies that the number
39:1731:86484:18:18:101:510:178:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:of workers per establishment should fall faster in manufacturing than in services. This is
39:1732:86574:18:18:101:510:160:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:confirmed in Figure 13(a), which plots the average number of workers per establishment
39:1733:86660:18:17:101:452:142:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:in the Business Dynamics Statistics (BDS) from the U.S. Census Bureau.
39:1734:86730:17:26:117:510:125:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:Furthermore, since the model generates vertical polarization by faster productivity
39:1735:86813:26:16:84:539:99:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f5:36Such selection does not matter for manual jobs (j= 0), because our discretized model assumes that all
39:1736:86916:16:11:71:540:83:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:workers contribute ̄h= 1 toward task-0 production regardless of theirh.  The increase in the manual job
39:1737:87019:11:0:71:340:72:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:wage is entirely due to the higher wage per effective skill,w0.

40:1747:87478:34:18:101:510:456:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:growth of routine jobs, which are more intensively used in manufacturing, the faster ver-
40:1748:87567:18:18:101:510:438:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:tical polarization in manufacturing is accompanied by higher productivity and output
40:1749:87651:18:17:101:510:420:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:growth among manufacturing establishments.  In Figure 13(b), we divide value-added
40:1750:87733:17:18:101:510:403:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:in the NIPA by the number of establishments in the BDS, which confirms the model
40:1751:87813:18:18:101:152:385:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:prediction.
40:1752:87824:18:18:117:510:367:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:An  inconsistency  is  that,  while  the  employment  share  of  managers  has  grown  in
40:1753:87913:18:18:101:510:349:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:the data, the number of employees per establishment overall has stayed more or less
40:1754:87996:18:18:101:510:331:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:constant throughout the observation period.  This suggests a need for modeling dif-
40:1755:88079:18:18:101:510:313:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:ferentiated managerial occupations and hierarchies of management, which can capture
40:1756:88162:18:33:101:288:295:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:the rise of middle managers since 1980.
40:1757:88201:33:18:101:510:262:12:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f5:Within-group wage inequalityBecause our quantitative model has a contin-
40:1758:88273:18:18:101:510:244:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f7:uum of skills and discrete occupations, it has implications for wage inequalitywithin
40:1759:88358:18:18:101:510:226:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:occupation  groups  as  well.   As  shown  in  Figure  14(a),  log  wage  variances  rose  sub-
40:1760:88453:18:18:101:510:208:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:stantially among managers, slightly among abstract workers, dropped among routine
40:1761:88534:18:18:101:427:190:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:workers and remained more or less constant among manual workers.
40:1762:88598:18:18:117:510:172:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:These qualitative patterns are replicated in our model, but the magnitudes of the
40:1763:88679:18:18:101:510:154:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:changes are too small compared to the data.  One way to address this is to increase
40:1764:88762:18:18:101:510:136:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:the variance of the underlying skill distribution over time, which we decided against
40:1765:88847:18:18:101:510:118:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:in order to isolate task specific forces:  We would be unable to separate the change in
40:1766:88934:18:18:101:510:100:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:the skill distribution that is task-specific as opposed to task-neutral, without further
40:1767:89022:18:0:101:297:82:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:assumptions on how skill is accumulated.
41:1768:89064:0:25:101:443:708:14:g_d0_f4:14:g_d0_f4:6.3    Polarization, Structural Change and TFP’s
41:1769:89112:25:18:101:510:683:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:The preceding subsections show that the model targeted only to within-sector employ-
41:1770:89196:18:18:101:510:665:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:ment shares delivers a good fit in terms of employment shares in the overall economy,
41:1771:89281:18:18:101:510:647:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:albeit less in terms of relative wages.  Other non-targeted moments such as establish-
41:1772:89367:18:18:101:510:629:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:ment size and wage inequality within occupations are also qualitatively consistent with
41:1773:89454:18:18:101:422:611:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:the data.  We now focus on sectoral employment shares and TFP’s.
41:1774:89518:18:18:117:510:593:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:To be more specific, we analyze the role of task-specific TFP’s on structural change
41:1775:89602:18:23:101:263:575:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:in relation to two counterfactuals.
41:1776:89637:23:18:107:510:552:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:(1)  We  restrict  all  task-specific  TFP  growth  to  be  equal,mj=mfor  allj,  and
41:1777:89722:18:18:127:510:534:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:instead let the exogenous sector-specific TFPs (Am,As) grow at ratesamandas,
41:1778:89798:18:18:127:510:516:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:respectively.  We jointly recalibratem,am, andasto match the empirical growth
41:1779:89875:18:18:127:511:498:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:rate of the aggregate and sectoral TFP’s from 1980 to 2010.  This version only
41:1780:89953:18:23:127:497:480:11:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:has exogenous sector-specific TFP growth but no task-specific TFP growth.37
41:1781:90028:23:18:107:511:457:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:(2)  We allow both exogenous task- and sector-specific TFP growth, and recalibrate
41:1782:90110:18:17:127:510:439:11:g_d0_f20:11:g_d0_f5:{mj}9j=z,0,am, andasto match the change in employment sharesandthe empir-
41:1783:90183:17:18:127:511:422:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:ical growth rates of the aggregate and sectoral TFP’s from 1980 to 2010.  Recall
41:1784:90263:18:18:127:511:404:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:that our benchmark calibration of Section 5 restrictedamandasto be 0 but did
41:1785:90339:18:23:127:248:386:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:not target sectoral TFPs.
41:1786:90364:23:18:101:510:363:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:In both cases, we keep all other parameters at their benchmark values of Tables 2-3,
41:1787:90448:18:18:101:308:345:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:and only recalibrate the TFP growth rates.
41:1788:90490:18:18:117:510:327:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:We focus on sectoral TFP’s since in our model structural change results from the
41:1789:90570:18:18:101:510:309:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:differential TFP growth between sectors—expressed in closed form in (19)—whether it
41:1790:90653:18:18:101:511:291:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:is exogenous (caused byamandas) or endogenous (as in Section 4.2).  The recalibrated
41:1791:90737:18:33:101:429:273:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:parameters for the counterfactual exercises are in Appendix Table 6.
41:1792:90805:33:18:101:510:240:12:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f5:TFP and output growthFigure 15 shows the paths of sectoral log TFP’s in the
41:1793:90880:18:18:101:510:222:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:data, in our benchmark calibration, and in the two counterfactuals.  By construction,
41:1794:90965:18:18:101:496:204:11:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:all four match aggregate TFP and GDP growth over time (Appendix Figure 24).38
41:1795:91042:18:18:117:510:186:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:Instead, we focus on the evolution of sectoral TFP’s.  Note that the calibrated task-
41:1796:91127:18:18:101:510:168:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:specific  TFP  growth  rates  are  higher  among  routine  jobs,  on  which  manufacturing
41:1797:91217:18:27:101:510:150:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:is more reliant.  Since the elasticity between sectors (-) is less than 1, we know from
41:1798:91304:27:16:84:540:123:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f5:37Sectoral  TFP  is  constructed  from  the  NIPA  accounts.   Real  value-added  and  capital  are  computed
41:1799:91413:16:12:72:540:107:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:via cyclical expansion from the industry accounts,  labor is computed from full-time equivalent persons in
41:1800:91519:12:8:72:379:95:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:production in NIPA Table 6, and TFP is the Solow residual by sector.
41:1801:91587:8:15:84:540:87:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f5:38Denoting  aggregate  TFP  asZt,  sinceYt=ZtKαt(labor  is  normalized  to  one)  and  we  plug  in  the
41:1802:91691:15:0:71:507:72:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:empirical values ofKtfor all calibrations, it is the same whether we match aggregate TFP or GDP.

42:1838:92158:33:18:101:510:470:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:Lemma 3 that the higher endogenous TFP growth in manufacturing leads to structural
42:1839:92240:18:18:101:510:452:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:change.   However,  our  benchmark  calibration  did  not  target  sectoral  TFP’s,  so  the
42:1840:92332:18:18:101:510:434:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:question is whether their growth can be explained by our benchmark with only task-
42:1841:92414:18:18:101:201:416:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:specific TFP growth.
42:1842:92434:18:18:117:510:398:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:In our benchmark, we overshoot the growth rate of manufacturing TFP by about
42:1843:92510:18:18:101:510:380:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:half a percentage point per annum, while undershooting the services TFP growth rate
42:1844:92593:18:18:101:510:362:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:by the same magnitude.  However, when we look at the growth rates of sectoral output
42:1845:92677:18:18:101:510:344:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:(Figure 25), these gaps nearly disappear.  This is because while the model assumes that
42:1846:92764:18:18:101:510:326:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:capital and labor input ratios are equal across sectors, as shown in (21), in the data
42:1847:92850:18:18:101:510:308:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:they are not. In fact, counterfactuals (1) and (2) in Appendix Figure 25 show that when
42:1848:92937:18:18:101:510:290:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:sectoral TFP growth is matched exactly, manufacturing output grows more slowly, and
42:1849:93020:18:18:101:510:272:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:services output more quickly, than in the data.  This implies that the capital input ratio
42:1850:93110:18:18:101:510:254:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:between manufacturing and services grew faster than the labor input ratio, although
42:1851:93193:18:18:101:510:236:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:the differences are small.  Consequently, once we include exogenous sector-specific TFP
42:1852:93280:18:17:101:510:218:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:growth  and  target  the  empirical  sectoral  TFP’s,  both  counterfactuals  under-predict
42:1853:93371:17:34:101:366:201:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:manufacturing output and over-predict services output.
42:1854:93425:34:18:101:510:167:12:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f5:Structural change and polarizationSince structural change from manufactur-
42:1855:93499:18:18:101:510:149:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:ing to services is solely determined by sectoral TFP ratios (Lemma 3), the fact that
42:1856:93583:18:17:101:510:131:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:endogenous  sectoral  TFP  growth  in  our  benchmark  closely  tracks  the  data  implies
42:1857:93673:17:18:101:510:114:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:that our model will also explain structural change in terms of employment shares.  As
42:1858:93758:18:18:101:510:96:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:shown in Figure 16, our benchmark overshoots the 13-percentage-point rise in the ser-
42:1859:93843:18:0:101:510:78:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:vice employment share in the data by 1 percentage point, while both counterfactuals

43:1878:94137:33:18:101:510:481:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:(1) and (2) undershoot by 3 percentage points.  Moreover, Appendix Figure 26 shows
43:1879:94219:18:18:101:510:463:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:that when we look at structural change within occupation groups, the benchmark out-
43:1880:94302:18:18:101:421:445:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:performs both counterfactuals (1) and (2), especially for managers.
43:1881:94369:18:18:117:510:427:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:The  benchmark  better  fits  employment  shares  than  the  counterfactuals,  despite
43:1882:94455:18:18:101:510:409:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:overshooting manufacturing’s relative TFP growth, because it has a better fit to sec-
43:1883:94540:18:18:101:510:391:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:toral output growth. As explained above, in the counterfactuals, sectoral output growth
43:1884:94627:18:18:101:510:373:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:is too low in manufacturing and too high in services.  To the extent that all structural
43:1885:94715:18:18:101:510:355:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:change in our model is due to differential growth in sectoral TFP’s, we do not intend
43:1886:94800:18:18:101:510:337:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:to emphasize too much that the benchmark explains employment shares better than
43:1887:94879:18:18:101:372:319:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:the counterfactuals that explicitly target sectoral TFP’s.
43:1888:94937:18:18:117:511:301:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:We do emphasize that exogenous changes in sectoral TFP’s cannot causewithin-
43:1889:95013:18:18:101:510:283:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:sector polarization.  We still investigate their effect on aggregate employment shares
43:1890:95099:18:18:101:510:265:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:by occupation (Lemma 4).  In Figure 17, we see that sectoral forces alone can account
43:1891:95184:18:18:101:510:247:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:for 15-20 percent of horizontal and vertical polarization in aggregate.  This is slightly
43:1892:95273:18:17:101:510:229:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:smaller  than  the  back-of-the-envelope  calculation  in  Section  4.3,  which  showed  that
43:1893:95366:17:18:101:510:212:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:the effect of sector-specific TFP’s on polarization is modest because both sectors use
43:1894:95452:18:18:101:161:194:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:routine jobs.
43:1895:95465:18:18:117:511:176:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:In contrast,  task-specific TFP’s (mj’s) can account for almost all of the changes
43:1896:95547:18:18:101:510:158:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:in  both  occupationalandsectoral  employment  shares.   At  first  glance,  it  may  seem
43:1897:95637:18:18:101:510:140:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:that the effect of task-specific TFP growth on sectoral TFP’s—which drive structural
43:1898:95721:18:18:101:510:122:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:change—should also be modest since services also benefits from the faster TFP growth
43:1899:95805:18:18:101:510:104:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:of  routine  tasks.   However,  differential  task-specific  TFP  growth  is  accompanied  by

44:1932:96483:34:18:101:257:445:11:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:reinforces the exogenous shifts.39
44:1933:96517:18:18:117:510:427:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:To summarize, task-specific TFP growth can more or less fully account for sectoral
44:1934:96599:18:17:101:510:409:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:TFP growth and  hence structural  change  observed between 1980  and 2010.  Due  to
44:1935:96682:17:18:101:510:392:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:the vertical and horizontal polarization induced by task-specific TFP growth, employ-
44:1936:96767:18:18:101:510:374:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:ment shifts to the sector that relies less on routine tasks and more on management.
44:1937:96850:18:18:101:510:356:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:Conversely, sector-specific productivities can only account for 15-20 percent of polar-
44:1938:96937:18:18:101:510:338:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:ization in the overall economy; more important, we have shown both analytically and
44:1939:97020:18:33:101:510:320:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:quantitatively that they cannot cause polarization within sectors, contrary to the data.
44:1940:97108:33:25:101:502:287:14:g_d0_f4:14:g_d0_f4:6.4    What Explains Task-Specific Productivity Growth?
44:1941:97163:25:18:101:510:262:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:Even  with  skill  selection,  horizontally  and  vertically  differentiated  occupations,  and
44:1942:97258:18:18:101:510:244:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:multiple sectors, Figure 18(a) shows that the bulk of the changes in occupational em-
44:1943:97343:18:18:101:510:226:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:ployment shares are directly accounted for by task-specific TFP’s, with a correlation
44:1944:97428:18:18:101:510:208:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:coefficient of -0.97.  This is also confirmed by the regression in the top panel of Ap-
44:1945:97515:18:18:101:510:190:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:pendix Table 7.  This leads us to conclude that in order to understand changes in the
44:1946:97600:18:18:101:497:172:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:employment structure, it is important to identify what these task-TFP’s represent.
44:1947:97682:18:18:117:510:154:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:How much of the variation in the task-specific TFP growth rates can be explained
44:1948:97762:18:18:101:510:136:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:by the widely-accepted routinization hypothesis—that routine jobs were more easily
44:1949:97844:18:19:101:510:118:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:automated  and  hence  now  employ  fewer  workers?   As  a  first  pass,  in  Figure  18(b)
44:1950:97936:19:16:84:539:99:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f5:39In Lemma 3, this is shown as the faster growth rates of the endogenous TFP components (ΠKi,ΠLi) in
44:1951:98036:16:11:72:540:83:10:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f5:services, which implies faster TFP growth inmanufacturing, given the expression for TFP (19) and assumed
44:1952:98140:11:0:71:289:72:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:values of elasticities.  Details are in Appendix B.5.

45:1980:99412:18:18:101:510:325:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:across occupations, and more strongly with the latter, there is much left to be explained.
45:1981:99502:18:18:101:484:307:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:Both the correlation andR2’s are still quite low, as shown in Appendix Table 7.
45:1982:99581:18:18:117:510:289:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:What about variables related to college education?  The skill-biased technological
45:1983:99663:18:18:101:510:271:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:change (SBTC) literature proxies skill by a (four-year) college degree—see Acemoglu
45:1984:99746:18:18:101:510:253:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:(2002) for a review.  As is evident from Figure 19(a),  neither the fraction of college
45:1985:99833:18:18:101:510:235:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:graduates within each occupation in 1980, nor the change in this fraction from 1980 to
45:1986:99919:18:18:101:510:217:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:2010, has much of a relationship with the task-specific TFP growth rates.  Although
45:1987:100002:18:18:101:510:199:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:not shown here,  the level in 1980 and the growth between 1980 and 2010 of within-
45:1988:100084:18:18:101:510:181:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:occupation college wage premium are not correlated with the task-specific TFP growth
45:1989:100168:18:17:101:510:163:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:either.  Moreover, as shown in Appendix Table 7, the correlation between task-specific
45:1990:100254:17:18:101:510:146:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:productivity growth and college-related variables isnegativeacross occupations; that is,
45:1991:100342:18:29:101:510:128:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:those occupations with more college graduates or those in which the college graduate
45:1992:100426:29:16:84:540:99:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f5:40Figure 21(b), replicated from Autor and Dorn (2013), shows where the top employment-weighted third
45:1993:100526:16:11:72:540:83:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:of occupations in terms of RTI are along the skill percentiles.  Because most routine jobs are found in the
45:1994:100633:11:0:72:535:72:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:middle, it is hypothesized and then formally tested that routinization causes (horizontal) job polarization.
46:1995:100759:0:0:102:284:563:2:g_d0_f45:2:g_d0_f45:LServAdminMachTransSalesTechMechMineProMSptMgr
46:1996:100805:0:8:180:278:700:2:g_d0_f45:2:g_d0_f45:CLG 1980 (right), corr: −.663TFP
46:1997:100837:8:152:180:238:692:2:g_d0_f45:2:g_d0_f45:CLG growth (right), corr: −.61
46:1998:100867:152:0:97:283:540:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:(a) Task-specific TFP Growth and College
46:1999:100913:0:0:334:526:563:2:g_d0_f46:2:g_d0_f46:LServAdminMachTransSalesTechMechMineProMSptMgr
46:2000:100959:0:8:410:521:700:2:g_d0_f46:2:g_d0_f46:Routine manual, corr:.797TFP
46:2001:100987:8:152:410:479:692:2:g_d0_f46:2:g_d0_f46:Manual interpersonal (−), corr: .767
46:2002:101023:152:27:327:516:540:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:(b) Task-specific TFP Growth and O*NET
46:2003:101061:27:11:114:498:513:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f11:Fig. 19:  Task TFP Growth, College Shares and O*NET-based Indices
46:2004:101126:11:12:72:540:502:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:In both Panel (a) and (b), gray bars are the task-TFP growth rates in percent per year.  Panel (a) has within-
46:2005:101236:12:12:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:occupation fraction of college graduates in 1980 and changes in this fraction across occupations.  Panel (b)
46:2006:101344:12:34:72:439:478:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:shows occupation-level routine-manual and manual-interpersonal indices in O*NET.
46:2007:101424:34:18:101:510:444:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:share  grew  the  fastest  in  fact  became  relatively  less  productive.   We  conclude  that
46:2008:101519:18:18:101:510:426:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:college-related variables do not explain the employment shifts across occupations and
46:2009:101604:18:18:101:510:408:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:sectors between 1980 and 2010.  This is in contrast to the preceding period:  Katz and
46:2010:101690:18:18:101:510:390:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:Murphy (1992) finds that college variables can account for the changes in occupational
46:2011:101776:18:18:101:510:372:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:employment shares from the early 1960s to mid-1980s, which our own empirical analysis
46:2012:101861:18:18:101:232:354:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:affirms (not reported here).
46:2013:101889:18:18:117:510:336:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:What  we  do  find,  however,  is  that  the  task-specific  TFP  growth  rates  correlate
46:2014:101979:18:18:101:510:318:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:strongly with sub-indices constructed by Acemoglu and Autor (2011) using O*NET,
46:2015:102058:18:18:101:510:300:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:rather  than  the  RTI  index  which  aggregates  over  them.   In  particular,  as  shown  in
46:2016:102152:18:18:101:510:282:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:Figure 19(b), the correlation of the task-TFP growth rates with routine-manual and
46:2017:102234:18:17:101:510:264:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:manual-interpersonal indices of occupations, the latter of which is not in RTI, is 0.80
46:2018:102321:17:18:101:511:247:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:and -0.77 respectively. Appendix Table 7 shows that theR2of the respective regressions
46:2019:102407:18:18:101:216:229:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:is high at 0.64 and 0.59.
46:2020:102432:18:18:117:510:211:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:We conclude that the technological progress since 1980 has predominantly enhanced
46:2021:102513:18:18:101:510:193:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:the productivity of those occupations that are heavy on routine-manual tasks but light
46:2022:102599:18:18:101:510:175:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:on interpersonal skills,  shrinking their employment shares and relative wages.  Since
46:2023:102685:18:18:101:510:157:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:routine physical activities are easy to automate but tasks requiring interpersonal skills
46:2024:102774:18:18:101:510:139:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:are not, this finding points to automation as the channel of task-specific technological
46:2025:102862:18:22:101:510:121:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:progress.41In this context, our findings are consistent with the routinization hypoth-
46:2026:102948:22:16:84:540:99:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f5:41As discussed at the beginning of Section 4, the effect of a change in factor-neutral task-TFP is quali-
46:2027:103053:16:11:72:540:83:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:tatively similar to that of a capital-augmenting change, which for example could have been modeled as the
46:2028:103158:11:0:72:498:72:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:fall in the price of task-specific capital (Goos et al., 2014) and directly interpreted as automation.
47:2029:103262:0:18:101:510:708:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:esis.  The unexplained part of task-specific TFP growth may also come from endoge-
47:2030:103344:18:18:101:510:690:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:nous changes in the distribution of manager and worker skills, heterogeneous degrees of
47:2031:103431:18:18:101:510:672:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:capital-labor substitutability across tasks,42and offshoring in an open economy setting,
47:2032:103519:18:39:101:287:654:11:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:all of which we have abstracted from.43
47:2033:103558:39:28:101:223:615:17:g_d0_f4:17:g_d0_f4:7    Conclusion
47:2034:103573:28:18:101:510:587:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:We presented a multi-sector task-based model in which individuals with heterogeneous
47:2035:103657:18:18:101:510:569:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:skills select into managers or workers, and workers further positively sort into differ-
47:2036:103745:18:18:101:510:551:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:entiated tasks.  It is a tractable yet powerful framework for studying the occupational,
47:2037:103833:18:18:101:510:533:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:industrial and organizational structure of an economy.  We fully characterize the equi-
47:2038:103920:18:18:101:510:515:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:librium and prove that task-specific technological progress for middle-skill jobs leads
47:2039:104007:18:18:101:510:497:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:to horizontal polarization, vertical polarization, and structural change.  Quantitatively,
47:2040:104097:18:18:101:510:479:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:we  show  that  task-specific  technological  progress  fully  accounts  for  all  of  the  above
47:2041:104194:18:18:101:510:461:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:phenomena  in  the  data,  unaided  by  sector-  or  factor-specific  technological  changes.
47:2042:104287:18:18:101:510:443:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:Consistent  with  the  model,  we  document  empirically  that  polarization  is  prevalent
47:2043:104378:18:18:101:510:425:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:within all sectors, including services, which suggests that trade is not the main driver
47:2044:104466:18:18:101:510:407:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:of polarization.  Both in the model and in the data, horizontal and vertical polariza-
47:2045:104552:18:18:101:510:389:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:tion is faster in manufacturing than in services,  causing structural change.  We also
47:2046:104638:18:17:101:510:371:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:show that task-specific technological changes since 1980 are fastest in occupations that
47:2047:104726:17:18:101:409:354:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:intensively use routine-manual tasks but not interpersonal skills.
47:2048:104792:18:18:117:510:336:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:Our model admits many useful extensions that have not been pursued here.  For in-
47:2049:104873:18:18:101:510:318:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:stance, one could embed individual skill dynamics to separate task productivity growth
47:2050:104959:18:18:101:510:300:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:from human capital accumulation, or include differentiated managerial tasks for a more
47:2051:105045:18:18:101:510:282:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:in-depth study of the organization of production by sector.  The latter will be espe-
47:2052:105130:18:18:101:510:264:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:cially useful for understanding assortative matching between managers and workers,
47:2053:105212:18:18:101:510:246:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:as well as for studying between- and within-firm inequality.  A quantitative analysis
47:2054:105297:18:18:101:510:228:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:with  more  than  two  sectors  would  facilitate  a  sharper  decomposition  of  occupation-
47:2055:105390:18:18:101:510:210:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:and  industry-specific  changes,  as  would  an  empirical  analysis  at  a  higher  frequency
47:2056:105484:18:18:101:510:192:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:(e.g.,  annual rather than decadal).  A multi-country extension is feasible,  which will
47:2057:105572:18:18:101:510:174:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:help analyze trade, offshoring, and foreign direct investment.  We are actively exploring
47:2058:105661:18:21:101:241:156:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:some of these exciting topics.
47:2059:105691:21:16:84:540:135:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f5:42That is, the elasticity of substitution between capital and workers’ human capital may vary across tasks.
47:2060:105798:16:12:72:540:119:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:This would be related but distinct from typical models of capital-skill complementarity in which the elasticity
47:2061:105909:12:8:72:514:107:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:varies directly by skill, e.g.  low- vs.  high-skill as in Krusell, Ohanian, R ́ıos-Rull, and Violante (2000).
47:2062:106019:8:16:84:540:99:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f5:43In an open economy setting, cheaper foreign labor would be qualitatively similar to higher productivity
47:2063:106124:16:12:72:540:83:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:at  the  task  level.   We  note  that,  although  not  shown  here,  the  task-TFP  growth  rates  are  only  weakly
47:2064:106241:12:0:72:506:71:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:correlated with occupations’ offshorability index constructed by Firpo, Fortin, and Lemieux (2011).

48:2081:106820:12:12:72:539:408:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:Osborne, 2005), are grouped into 11 occupation groups in order of theirocc1990ddcode.  All self-employed
48:2082:106924:12:12:72:540:396:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:workers are classified as managers.  All other occupation groups correspond to their 1-digit census occupation
48:2083:107034:12:12:72:540:384:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:group except for management support, technicians and sales.  Groups are presented in their (contiguous),
48:2084:107138:12:12:72:540:372:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:ascending order of their codes, excluding agricultural occupations 473–498 which are dropped.  In the main
48:2085:107244:12:30:72:445:360:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:text, occupation groups are presented in ascending order of skill (mean hourly wage).
48:2086:107329:30:18:117:510:330:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:We use the 5% census samples from IPUMS USA. We drop military, unpaid family
48:2087:107405:18:18:101:510:312:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:workers,  and individuals who were in correctional or mental facilities.  We also drop
48:2088:107491:18:18:101:419:294:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:workers who work either in an agricultural occupation or industry.
48:2089:107557:18:17:117:510:276:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:For  each  individual,  (annual)  employment  is  defined  as  the  product  of  weeks
48:2090:107643:17:18:101:510:259:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:worked  times  usual  weekly  hours,  weighted  by  census  sampling  weights.    Missing
48:2091:107732:18:18:101:510:241:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:usual  weekly  hours  are  imputed  by  hours  worked  last  week  when  possible.   Missing
48:2092:107824:18:18:101:510:223:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:observations  are  imputed  from  workers  in  the  same  year-occupation-education  cell
48:2093:107913:18:18:101:510:205:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:with 322 occupations×6 hierarchical education categories:  less than high school, some
48:2094:107999:18:18:101:433:187:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:high school, high school, some college, college, and more than college.
48:2095:108070:18:18:117:510:169:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:Hourly wages are computed as annual labor income divided by annual employment
48:2096:108147:18:18:101:510:151:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:at  the  individual  level.   Hence  while  employment  shares  include  the  self-employed,
48:2097:108239:18:22:101:510:133:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:hourly  wages  do  not  include  self-employment  income.44We  correct  for  top-coded
48:2098:108325:22:16:84:540:111:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f5:44While  we  have  only  considered  labor  income  in  the  paper,  we  have  conducted  robustness  checks  by
48:2099:108437:16:12:72:540:95:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:including business income as well.  Hourly business income is defined similarly as hourly wages.  We also
48:2100:108542:12:11:72:540:83:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:separately corrected for top-coding (the top-codes for labor and business income differ) and bottom-coded
48:2101:108647:11:0:72:158:72:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:in a similar fashion.

58:2752:125856:18:18:117:510:475:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:Why  structural  change  cannot  be  overturned,  as  explained  in  the  text,  is  also

58:2755:126123:18:18:101:511:421:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:overturn the direction of structural change in (51a) as long as- <1.  Equations (50)-

58:2757:126296:17:38:101:198:386:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f5:of capital and labor.
